  <a href="#other_reviews" class="actionLinkLite votes">
      <span itemprop="ratingCount" title="1715" class="value-title">1,715 Ratings</span>
</a>  <span class="greyText"> · </span>
  <a href="#other_reviews" class="actionLinkLite">
      <span class="count"><span title="250" class="value-title">250</span></span>
      Reviews

<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer7834988598485832296">Gappers will get your goat. Literally. If you don't brush them off and return them to the ocean, whence they arrive every day, these bright orange, many-eyed creatures will cover your goats, and the goats will stop giving milk. <br />In a village called Frip, goat's milk was the entire economy. Three families lived there--the Romos, the Ronsens, and a little girl named Capable a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7834988598485832296">Gappers will get your goat. Literally. If you don't brush them off and return them to the ocean, whence they arrive every day, these bright orange, many-eyed creatures will cover your goats, and the goats will stop giving milk. <br />In a village called Frip, goat's milk was the entire economy. Three families lived there--the Romos, the Ronsens, and a little girl named Capable and her widowed father, who wanted everything to remain the same. It didn't. One day, the Gappers, despite an average IQ of 3.7 ( .02), decided for a good reason to concentrate on Capable's goats. Oh, how the Romos and Ronsens turned their backs on the gapper-ridden Capable! Oh, how they indeed lorded it over her! What kinds of creatures are we, one wonders, when such selfishness so often springs up so spontaneously among us? <br />And, given the coldness of her neighbors' shoulders, what will Capable do about her Gapper plague, as her share of the economy dries up? Literally. The Very Persistent Gappers of Frip, with a brilliant story by award-ridden short-story master George Saunders and fifty-two haunting and hilarious illustrations by bestseller-plagued artist/author Lane Smith, answers that question. In doing so it tells a tale as ancient as the Bible and as modern as a memo from the Federal Reserve Board. And funnier than both--which isn't saying all that much, admittedly. You don't get to laugh and gaze in visual awe and pleasure all that often when the Golden Rule comes under such serious attack and such staunch defense as it did in Frip. <br />An adult story for children, a children's story for adults, an earthlings' story for aliens, an oceanside fable for the landlocked, a capitalist tool foranarchists, a fish story for loaves, The Very Persistent Gappers of Frip represents the classic instant of two young geniuses colliding and colluding. The result is--what else?--an instant classic!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7834988598485832296">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('edition_152857').observe('prototip:shown', function () {

          });

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1460226864">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1460226864" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3978225-carol" class="left imgcol" title="Carol. "><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1307934801p2/3978225.jpg" alt="Carol. " /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1460226864?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 03, 2016</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3978225-carol" class="user" title="Carol. ">Carol. </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/28748.The_Very_Persistent_Gappers_of_Frip" title="Hardcover 9781932416374" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of The Stinky Cheese Man</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=female-lead" class="actionLinkLite">female-lead</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=animals-and-people" class="actionLinkLite">animals-and-people</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=children-s" class="actionLinkLite">children-s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1460226864">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9937251969486241553">Poor Gappers! They just want to climb on the goats and shriek with happiness.<br /><br />Poor goats! If they can’t sleep from the shrieking, then they can’t make milk!<br /><br /><img width="200" height="200" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1451800905i/17606021.jpg" /><br /><br />A girl named Capable and her father have been trying to survive since Capable’s mother died earlier this year. Dad really would like things to stay exactly as they were that day, including the sun staying up and all his meals made of white food. Poor Capable has her hands full carrying the gappers back to the sea and preparing a chalk mixture</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9937251969486241553">Poor Gappers! They just want to climb on the goats and shriek with happiness.<br /><br />Poor goats! If they can’t sleep from the shrieking, then they can’t make milk!<br /><br /><img width="200" height="200" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1451800905i/17606021.jpg" /><br /><br />A girl named Capable and her father have been trying to survive since Capable’s mother died earlier this year. Dad really would like things to stay exactly as they were that day, including the sun staying up and all his meals made of white food. Poor Capable has her hands full carrying the gappers back to the sea and preparing a chalk mixture to make Dad’s meals look white. One day, the smartest gapper realizes that Capable’s goats are fifteen feet closer than the goats of the other two families of Frip, so they descend en mass to happily visit Capable’s goats. What will she do? Will her neighbors help?<br /><br />A clever enough tale, TVPGoF feels a little arch, a little <em>self-congratulatory,</em> a little too <em>hip.</em> There’s something about it that screams for attention despite lacking the charm of other children’s books (<em>Fly by Night, The Girl Who Circumnavigated…</em>). I guess I tentatively like the moral, although the narrative feels surprisingly condemning in the process of getting there. There is a surprising obstacle or two before it resolves as expected.<br /><br /><img width="200" height="200" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1451800905i/17606022._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />But what’s a children’s book without illustrations? And like <em>The Stinky Cheese </em>book, I’m not sure these pictures are designed to appeal to younger eyes. People resemble the distorted figures of <em>A Nightmare Before Christmas.</em> It’s a muted, fall palette and with detail more appropriate to a demographic that can appreciate the weathered patina on a house, or the jail-like costuming of the movers. A far cry, to be sure, from the books where a picture brings something about the story to be discovered. Still, the gappers manage to be almost cute, facilitating the idea that they are just animals doing their thing, and the goats are very goat-like. Artistically, it is well done, just not the kind of style that appeals to me personally.<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1451800905i/17606023._SX540_.jpg" /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9937251969486241553">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_433479880">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/433479880" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2508169-paquita-maria-sanchez" class="left imgcol" title="Paquita Maria Sanchez"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1417909898p2/2508169.jpg" alt="Paquita Maria Sanchez" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/433479880?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 31, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2508169-paquita-maria-sanchez" class="user" title="Paquita Maria Sanchez">Paquita Maria Sanchez</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/952202.The_Very_Persistent_Gappers_Of_Frip" title="Hardcover 9780747550419" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2508169-paquita-maria-sanchez?shelf=pitcher-books" class="actionLinkLite">pitcher-books</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2508169-paquita-maria-sanchez?shelf=literature" class="actionLinkLite">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer433479880">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7636901967794529338">Saunders, you pinko! You make me want to get knocked up just so I can have a small, impressionable mind to mold with your morals. Pick this up if you want your child to learn how wrong it is for a few to benefit from the misfortunes of many, that sustainability should be sought in place of convenience, that if you're only out for yourself you are bound to lose because we're all in this together and all that blardy blardy, that a friend in need is a friend indeed, that boys who like girls should</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7636901967794529338">Saunders, you pinko! You make me want to get knocked up just so I can have a small, impressionable mind to mold with your morals. Pick this up if you want your child to learn how wrong it is for a few to benefit from the misfortunes of many, that sustainability should be sought in place of convenience, that if you're only out for yourself you are bound to lose because we're all in this together and all that blardy blardy, that a friend in need is a friend indeed, that boys who like girls should like girls who wear overalls and fall down a lot and paint all their food white and teach themselves to fish rather than purdy gals who stand real still and quiet and poised and boring, that it's important to always be open to new ideas rather than stuck in some backwoods, biblical bullshit, that gappers would make insanely adorable pets whose shrieking surely couldn't be much louder than my roommate's pug's motorboat snoring, and that Lane Smith should design a tattoo for me. Your child should know all of that. <br /><br />I would provide a bunch of Smith's beautimousful kiddie illustrations for your previewing pleasure, but GIS is sort of failing me today. On the bright side, it gave me the super swank image below which linked to a Saunders interview where he admits that the celebrity sex tape of his dreams would involve Abraham Lincoln and Abraham Lincoln fully clothed in matching garb, just having a conversation. Love that man. Saunders, that is. <br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s89.photobucket.com/albums/k226/krististeffen/?action=view&amp;current=saunders.jpg">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="Photobucket" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380394206r/758975._SX540_SY540_.jpg" /></a><br /><br />And now, a word from your local politician:<br /><br /><em>"Well, I'm against it," he said. "If we haven't done it before, it stands to reason that this is the first time we've done it, which means that, relative to what we've done in the past, this is different, which I am very much against, as I always have been, as you well know. I have consistently been very consistent about this."</em><br /><br />Blacklisted. <br /><br />Gapper gapper gapper gapper.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s89.photobucket.com/albums/k226/krististeffen/?action=view&amp;current=gapper.jpg">
    <img class="escapedImg" alt="Photobucket" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380394206r/758976._SX540_SY540_.jpg" /></a>
</blockquote></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7636901967794529338">...more</a>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=children" class="actionLinkLite">children</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer400106099">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17993611378052295454">Welcome to Frip, population 4 adults, 5 children, 30 goats and approximately fifteen hundred gappers.<br /><br />Gappers are bright orange baseball-sized critters who look something like a cross between a blowfish and a many-eyed Pac-Man. They exist seemingly only to perch on goats and shriek, causing said goats to stop giving milk and collapse from nervous exhaustion.<br /><br />You can probably see that this could be a problem for the good citizens of Frip.<br /><br />And thereby hangs a tale with all the makings of a classic. T</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17993611378052295454">Welcome to Frip, population 4 adults, 5 children, 30 goats and approximately fifteen hundred gappers.<br /><br />Gappers are bright orange baseball-sized critters who look something like a cross between a blowfish and a many-eyed Pac-Man. They exist seemingly only to perch on goats and shriek, causing said goats to stop giving milk and collapse from nervous exhaustion.<br /><br />You can probably see that this could be a problem for the good citizens of Frip.<br /><br />And thereby hangs a tale with all the makings of a classic. There is a brave young heroine, dithering ineffectual adults straight out of a <a title="Roald Dahl" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4273.Roald_Dahl">Roald Dahl</a> book, and a warm and fuzzy moral about neighbor helping neighbor, and everyone working together to achieve a goal.<br /><br />And dammit! Why has no one heard of this book?<br /><br />I'm not sure I've ever seen such a smooth marriage of narrative and illustration. <a title="Lane Smith" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/23573.Lane_Smith">Lane Smith</a>'s artwork is the perfect compliment for this oddball story - murky, yet playful, textured, creepy and humorous. <br /><br />Seek this one out. It is a treasure waiting to be discovered.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17993611378052295454">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer508472378">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2631983579815474437">(Reprinted from the Chicago Center for Literature and Photography [cclapcenter.com]. I am the original author of this essay, as well as the owner of CCLaP; it is not being reprinted illegally.)<br /><br />I had the pleasure of getting to talk with legendary author George Saunders <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.cclapcenter.com/2013/01/cclap_podcast_96_author_george.html">for CCLaP's podcast last week</a>, a rare treat given how in demand he is on this latest tour even among the major media; but that meant I had to do some serious cramming in the few weeks leading up to our talk, in that (I guiltily con</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2631983579815474437">(Reprinted from the Chicago Center for Literature and Photography [cclapcenter.com]. I am the original author of this essay, as well as the owner of CCLaP; it is not being reprinted illegally.)<br /><br />I had the pleasure of getting to talk with legendary author George Saunders <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.cclapcenter.com/2013/01/cclap_podcast_96_author_george.html">for CCLaP's podcast last week</a>, a rare treat given how in demand he is on this latest tour even among the major media; but that meant I had to do some serious cramming in the few weeks leading up to our talk, in that (I guiltily confess) I only became aware of his existence a month ago, because of a passionate recommendation from my friend and Chicago science-fiction author Mark R. Brand, with Saunders' new book, tour, and interview opportunity being merely a fortuitous coincidence. And that's because the vast majority of Saunders' output has been short stories, while regulars know that my own reading habits veer almost 100 percent to full novels, which means he's simply and unfortunately been off my radar this whole time; but of course I'm happy to make room in my life for exquisite short-fiction writers once I learn about them (see for example my revelation after reading John Cheever for the first time a few years ago), which means that I tore through all seven books now of his career in just a few weeks recently, so I thought I'd get one large essay posted here about all of them at once, instead of doing a separate small review for each book.<br /><br />And indeed, as I mentioned during the podcast as well, like Cheever I think Saunders' work is going to be at its most powerful once his career is over, and all the stories collected into one giant volume that a person reads all at once, instead of debating the merits of one individual collection over another. And in fact this is something else I said in the podcast, that I find it fun to think of Saunders' stories as essentially interchangeable tales in one big comic-book-style shared universe, albeit the most f-cked-up shared universe you'll ever spend time in: a possibly post-apocalyptic America, although whether through slow erosion or one big doomsday event is hard to determine, where the only businesses that still thrive are outlandish theme parks designed for the amusement of the now "natural betters" of our new Mad Max society, and staffed by the permanent class of have-nots which now includes a large population of genetically modified freaks, a place where ghosts are real and magic exists and the new normal is extreme cruelty at all times for all other humans left in the wreckage of a crumbled United States. And so if you look at the four story collections that Saunders has now put out -- 1996's <em>CivilWarLand in Bad Decline</em>, 2000's <em>Pastoralia</em>, 2006's <em>In Persuasion Nation</em> and this year's <em>Tenth of December</em> -- you'll see that the vast majority of all these pieces fit at least somewhat into the general paradigm just described, although with others that are much more realistic in tone but still with the same unbelievable cruelty and darkness, many of them set among racially tense situations in eroding post-industrial cities.<br /><br />Yeah, sounds like a big barrel of laughs, right? And in fact this was the biggest surprise for me as well when first reading them, that Saunders is not just on the stranger side of the bizarro* subgenre, but is one of the most wrist-slashingly depressing authors you will ever find, yet this Guggenheim and MacArthur grant winner is regularly on the bestseller lists, has appeared on David Letterman and <em>The Daily Show</em>, gets published on a steady basis in such hugely mainstream magazines as <em>The New Yorker</em> and <em>GQ</em>, and is adored by literally millions of fans out there, many of whom would never open the cover of a book from Eraserhead Press to save their life. And that's because Saunders never talks about these things specifically to be depressing, but rather as a way of highlighting how important simple humanity is to our lives, the simple act of being humane and optimistic about the world, which he does not by writing about the humane acts themselves but what a world without them would look like. And that's a clever and admirable thing to do, because it means he sneaks in sideways to the points he wants to make, not beating us over the head but forcing us to really stop and think about what he's truly trying to say, to examine why we get so upset when this fundamental humanity is missing from the stories we're reading. Ultimately Saunders believes in celebrating life, in trying to be as helpful and open-minded to strangers as you can, in being as positive about the world at large as you can stand; but like the Existentialists of Mid-Century Modernism, he examines this subject by looking at worst-case scenarios, and by showing us what exactly we miss out of in life when this positivity and love is gone.<br /><br />*(For those who are new to CCLaP, "bizarro" is a hard-to-define term but one we reference here a lot; also sometimes known as "gonzo" fiction, sometimes as "The New Weird," a lot of it comes from either the wackier or more prurient edges of such existing genres as science-fiction, horror and erotica, while some of it is more like Hunter S. Thompson or William S. Burroughs, a conceptual cloud of strangeness that has a <em>huge</em> cult following in the world of basement presses and genre conventions, as well as such literary social networks as Goodreads.com. If you want to think of famous examples, think of people like Kathy Acker, Mark Leyner, Will Self, Chuck Palahniuk, Blake Butler, China Mieville…and, uh, George Saunders!)<br /><br />Now, of course, in all honesty, there are also a few clunkers scattered here and there in these collections as well, which is simply to be expected in a career that now spans twenty years; and when it comes to the small number of other books he's put out besides story collections, I have to confess that I found those to be a much iffier proposition. For example, there's the 2000 children's book <em>The Very Persistent Gappers of Frip</em>, cute enough but as inessential to an adult as any children's book is; then there's his one collection of nonfiction essays, 2007's <em>The Braindead Megaphone</em>, an uneven compilation of random pieces which includes some real gems (one of the best being that <em>GQ</em> piece mentioned, where Saunders is sent George-Plimpton-style to Dubai, and instead of the usual decrying of the ultra-rich he is surprisingly charmed by all the vacationing middle-class families), but that has an equal amount of throwaway pieces done for highly specific commissions; and then there's the only stand-alone fiction book of his career so far, the 2005 novella <em>The Brief and Frightening Reign of Phil</em>, which I have to confess is the only thing of Saunders' career that I actively disliked -- written in the middle of the Bush atrocities, it's obviously an attempt to do an <em>Animal Farm</em>-style satire about those years, but is labored in its execution, too on the nose, and in general has too much of a "quirky for the sake of being quirky" vibe, the exact thing that can most quickly kill a piece of bizarro fiction. (But then again, we perhaps shouldn't blame Saunders for this; as I've talked about many times here in the past, it seems that no indignant artist was able to write satirically about Bush in the middle of the Bush Years without producing an overly obvious ranting screed, whether that's Saunders or George Clooney or Michael Moore or Robert Redford. No wonder no good books about Nazis came out until after World War Two; as we all learned in the early 2000s, it's nearly impossible to actually live under a fascist regime and also be subtle and clever in your critique of it.)<br /><br />But those are all small quibbles, of course; Saunders' bread and butter is in his short fiction, and I'm convinced that he will eventually be known as one of the best short-fiction authors in history, joining a surprisingly small list that includes such luminaries as Cheever, William Faulkner, Eudora Welty, GK Chesterton and more. Plus, as a fan of edgy and strange work, I'm thrilled that a guy like Saunders is out there, serving as a gateway of sorts between mainstream society and an entire rabbithole of basement-press bizarro titles that's just waiting for newly inspired fans to tumble down. If you're going to pick up your first Saunders book soon, go ahead and pick up the newest, <em>Tenth of December</em>, because it's just as good as all the others and particularly easy to find right now; but I also encourage you to dig deeper into this remarkable author's career, and to see just how far he'll pull you into the murky depths of ambiguous morality before coming bobbing back to the surface. It's been a true treat to become a fan of his work this year, and I urge you to become one as well.<br /><br />Out of 10 (<em>Tenth of December</em>): <strong>9.6</strong></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2631983579815474437">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14959751225025013562">Read this for the third time (it’s 84 pages, illustrated) and this time I did it aloud to Luke. It’s the story of a girl named Capable living in the town of Frip who’s exhausted from her job of brushing off gappers from her goats on a daily basis. Gappers are baseball-sized, multi-eyed creatures that adhere themselves to goats and then shriek joyfully. The goats get put out. Capable’s neighbors are buffoons and she comes up with a plan. It’s a bizarre and occasionally funny tale which has the im</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14959751225025013562">Read this for the third time (it’s 84 pages, illustrated) and this time I did it aloud to Luke. It’s the story of a girl named Capable living in the town of Frip who’s exhausted from her job of brushing off gappers from her goats on a daily basis. Gappers are baseball-sized, multi-eyed creatures that adhere themselves to goats and then shriek joyfully. The goats get put out. Capable’s neighbors are buffoons and she comes up with a plan. It’s a bizarre and occasionally funny tale which has the importance of being neighborly as a lesson learned. George Saunders is more well-known for his offbeat short story collections (I’ve read ‘em). This book is gorgeously illustrated by Lane Smith (of <em>Stinky Cheese Man</em> fame).<br /><br />I re-read this again on December 3rd, 2012 to both my kids. </span>

      <a href="/user/show/2527265-david" class="user" title="David">David</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer9068692416607248862">I had a hard time finding this book at the Washington, D.C., public library because, although it is about a resourceful little girl and has whimsical illustrations, it is shelved with the novels for grown-ups. <br /><br />Has anyone out there every actually read this to or with an actual child? What was the child's reaction?<br /><br />Saunders is sort of literary flavor-of-the-month now. I see serious people with goatees and/or tattoos reading his latest, <em> <a title="Tenth of December by George Saunders" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13641208.Tenth_of_December">Tenth of December</a> </em>, in coffee houses. It's encouraging in thes</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9068692416607248862">I had a hard time finding this book at the Washington, D.C., public library because, although it is about a resourceful little girl and has whimsical illustrations, it is shelved with the novels for grown-ups. <br /><br />Has anyone out there every actually read this to or with an actual child? What was the child's reaction?<br /><br />Saunders is sort of literary flavor-of-the-month now. I see serious people with goatees and/or tattoos reading his latest, <em>
  <a title="Tenth of December by George Saunders" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13641208.Tenth_of_December">Tenth of December</a>
</em>, in coffee houses. It's encouraging in these times to see anyone reading anything anywhere, so no complaints, no remarks about goatees or tattoos.<br /><br />As remarked here on Goodreads, there's a certain world view evident in this book that is likely to please the goatee-and-tattoo crowd. One reviewer seemed to think this book displayed anti-Christian bias because one of the (temporary) villains falls into the still-very-common trap (common across all religions) of attributing good fortune to God's favor. It seems barely worth mentioning that people who do this are really missing something very basic about the religious life, even if they go to a place of worship every week.<br /><br />The religious are touchy. Compare: Elsewhere in this story, Saunders mocks the eminently mockable advice to “work smarter, not harder”. You don't see a lot of management consultants getting all stroppy on Goodreads about it.<br /><br />I guess I understand the D.C. public library's decision to put this in the section with the books for grown up. They don't want a rushed parent absently pulling this off the shelf for their child's bedtime reading one week and returning enraged the next, asking what kind of morally polluting secular humanist filth the pub. lib. is peddling these days. I suppose that could happen. Even in Washington DC.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_48227849">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/48227849" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/65207-shannon" class="left imgcol" title="Shannon"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1414992428p2/65207.jpg" alt="Shannon" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/48227849?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 04, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/65207-shannon" class="user" title="Shannon">Shannon</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/28748.The_Very_Persistent_Gappers_of_Frip" title="Hardcover 9781932416374" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/65207-shannon?shelf=lulz" class="actionLinkLite">lulz</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer48227849">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18099003177653149771">While the underlying message behind this allegory is not the most original, the premise, and relationship between the goats/gappers/3 families in the town of Frip, is. Plus the gapeprs in general are rather hilarious, though I felt bad for the goats (The gappers, little tennis-ball-like eye-covered beasts love the goats and shriek with joy when upon them, but it makes the goats lie down, sad). Anyway.. the illustrations are AWESOME and elevate the book to a whole new level of enjoyable-ness. Als</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18099003177653149771">While the underlying message behind this allegory is not the most original, the premise, and relationship between the goats/gappers/3 families in the town of Frip, is. Plus the gapeprs in general are rather hilarious, though I felt bad for the goats (The gappers, little tennis-ball-like eye-covered beasts love the goats and shriek with joy when upon them, but it makes the goats lie down, sad). Anyway.. the illustrations are AWESOME and elevate the book to a whole new level of enjoyable-ness. Also: Capable is an awesome name. Not subtle but that wasn't the aim, of course. <br /><br />This book kind of makes me want to give Saunders a second chance- I was kind of let down by <a title="The Braindead Megaphone by George Saunders" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/381960.The_Braindead_Megaphone">The Braindead Megaphone</a>, but maybe he's just one of those people who is better at fiction than editorializing. <br /><br />Overall, I recommend it for a quick, amusing read. I'm not sure if it's intended as a children's book or not: it could work either way..<br /><br />Oh also there was a cute poke at gender roles, with two little girls who looked very pretty if they stood very still, and did so, because they wanted to look cute for boys. I thought that was a subtle and amusing message.<br /><br /><br />Edit: Actually, thinking more about it, I think this book managed to bring up quite a few complex philosophical issues (impressive consideing how short it is), such as: do people DESERVE their ill/good fortune, should you share/help your neighbors, etc etc. Actually pretty thought provoking, if you're that kind of person. Or you can read it as a straight-up, literal story, perhaps to a child, and it would still be enjoyable.<br /></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/28748.The_Very_Persistent_Gappers_of_Frip" title="Hardcover 9781932416374" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/122610-jeffrey?shelf=general-reading" class="actionLinkLite">general-reading</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer11111184">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8717039339263681309">Is this a kid's book? Maybe, but I wouldn't recommend that a very young child read it, or even look at the pictures - it could be scary. Adults will find it thought provoking. <br /><br />In Frip, people act with blissful ignorance, accepting the world as it is as though they understand it, but they don't. Their ignorance leads to catastrophe for a few of them, and the thoughtful determination of one character leads her to success, all through trial and error. <br /><br />In today's modern world, science tries to answe</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8717039339263681309">Is this a kid's book? Maybe, but I wouldn't recommend that a very young child read it, or even look at the pictures - it could be scary. Adults will find it thought provoking. <br /><br />In Frip, people act with blissful ignorance, accepting the world as it is as though they understand it, but they don't. Their ignorance leads to catastrophe for a few of them, and the thoughtful determination of one character leads her to success, all through trial and error. <br /><br />In today's modern world, science tries to answer the questions for us about why things are the way they are, but when it comes to our personal success and social position, many of us assume that we somehow "deserve" our fate, good or bad, and that others who suffer misfortune somehow deserve that as well. Our sophisticated social psychology may be no better than the simplemindedness of the residents of Frip in answering these questions.<br /><br />The author uses allegory and simple stories to help us see our own weaknesses and foibles. He uncovers our assumptions about the world by finding the lowest common denominator and stripping away all of the familiar trappings that distract us from the truth.<br /><br />Actually this is a product of a very fertile imagination. No wonder he won a MacArthur grant! <br /><br />It is refreshing and fun stuff, even if you don't give it a moment of deep thought. (I think I need to read it to a kid....)</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8717039339263681309">...more</a>

          </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5387998-eric?shelf=read-to-the-kids" class="actionLinkLite">read-to-the-kids</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer228759289">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1191816790654101837">I already read this book to my son earlier this year, and I was pleasantly surprised when he asked me to read it again. He almost never asks me to read chapter books to him again, and the fact that he chose this bizarre modern folk tale leads me to believe he may be a chip off the old block.<br /><br />Gappers are little spiky orange creatures that crawl out of the sea and attach themselves to goats. They love goats very much and scream in ecstasy when they are attached to one. Neither the goats nor their o</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1191816790654101837">I already read this book to my son earlier this year, and I was pleasantly surprised when he asked me to read it again. He almost never asks me to read chapter books to him again, and the fact that he chose this bizarre modern folk tale leads me to believe he may be a chip off the old block.<br /><br />Gappers are little spiky orange creatures that crawl out of the sea and attach themselves to goats. They love goats very much and scream in ecstasy when they are attached to one. Neither the goats nor their owners like the gappers, and it's up to the children of this three family town to brush the gappers off their goats. Our heroine, a young girl named Capable, does not enjoy this life. When an exceptionally smart gapper figures out that her goats are the easiest to love, <br /><br />Saunders, who usually writes for adults, has created a smart, funny story that should appeal to people of all ages. I'd recommend TVPGoF to anyone and everyone.</span>

              <a href="/review/list/6504531-francesca-brunner?shelf=children-s-books" class="actionLinkLite">children-s-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1464791028">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12997787778021255263">One of my absolute favourite children's books, a definite gem. From the cover design to the format, with amazing illustrations by Lane Smith (James &amp; the Giant Peach and Where is Art?), this is very compelling visually. The title of this book is a little different, mysterious even, but like a great indie movie, it pulls you in. The story centers around Capable, a quiet, self-contained heroine, who lives with her widowed father in Frip. The 'gappers' of the title are Capable's nemeses, and ma</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12997787778021255263">One of my absolute favourite children's books, a definite gem. From the cover design to the format, with amazing illustrations by Lane Smith (James &amp; the Giant Peach and Where is Art?), this is very compelling visually. The title of this book is a little different, mysterious even, but like a great indie movie, it pulls you in. The story centers around Capable, a quiet, self-contained heroine, who lives with her widowed father in Frip. The 'gappers' of the title are Capable's nemeses, and make her life impossible by attaching themselves to her goats, which refuse to produce milk. Capable's father is still grieving her mother's death, and is no help at all. Her neighbors (hilariously depicted by both writer and illustrator) are both smug and superior, and despite their superficial piety, don't feel compelled to help Capable at all. So she figures out a way to survive, selling the goats and learning to fish. The neighbors, meanwhile, are still struggling with the gappers. Capable is a successful fisherman, but after a good catch, she has a decision to make. Should she turn her back on the uncaring neighbors and enjoy a nice dinner with her father? Or should she open her home to them, and invite them to share her catch? As readers, we get to share Capable's dilemma, and find out the results of her decision. <br />Although I have this book on my children's bookshelf, this one is for all ages. Can be read over and over, with multiple layers of understanding. Love it.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12997787778021255263">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_421812522">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/421812522" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/13140107-jennifer-lavonier" class="left imgcol" title="Jennifer Lavonier"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1348938639p2/13140107.jpg" alt="Jennifer Lavonier" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/421812522?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 25, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/13140107-jennifer-lavonier" class="user" title="Jennifer Lavonier">Jennifer Lavonier</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/28748.The_Very_Persistent_Gappers_of_Frip" title="Hardcover 9781932416374" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer421812522'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer421812522">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11938982199324856513">The Very Persistent Gappers of Frip is a cautionary tale; one which demonstrates that you should not take joy in another’s misfortune, for you may someday find yourself in a similar position.<br /><br />Frip is a small seaside town. So small in fact, that only three families live there—our young hero Capable and her widowed father, the Romos and the Ronsens. Though they live by the sea, their livelihood is reliant on their goats, for the goats make milk that the families can sell. <br /><br />Gappers, those meddlesome</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11938982199324856513">The Very Persistent Gappers of Frip is a cautionary tale; one which demonstrates that you should not take joy in another’s misfortune, for you may someday find yourself in a similar position.<br /><br />Frip is a small seaside town. So small in fact, that only three families live there—our young hero Capable and her widowed father, the Romos and the Ronsens. Though they live by the sea, their livelihood is reliant on their goats, for the goats make milk that the families can sell. <br /><br />Gappers, those meddlesome creatures, love goats more than anything else. They attach themselves to the goats, and then proceed to emit a high-pitched squeal of pleasure. This is troublesome to the goats; it causes them lose sleep, lose weight, and eventually, stop giving milk.<br /><br />It is the job of the children to brush the gappers off their family’s goats, gather the gappers in sacks, and empty the sacks into the sea. It takes the gappers 3 hours to crawl out of the ocean, up the cliff and back onto the goats. Therefore, the children must perform this task eight times a day. Every day. <br /><br />Capable’s house is closest to the sea so the gappers always reach her goats first. When one marginally smarter gapper realizes that they can get all the goat they need from this one family, things become unbalanced. <br /><br />The Romos and Ronsens couldn’t be more pleased to be relieved of their brushing duties and are all too vocal about it. Now Capable is doing the work of three families all by herself and she cannot keep up. Despite her father’s wishes, Capable asks the neighbors for help. Not only are they not willing to help, but they also blame Capable for bringing this plague upon herself! In fact, they’ve moved their houses farther away from hers so as not to “catch” whatever it is she has that has brought all the gappers to her yard, instead of spreading out over all three yards. <br /><br />Capable can take no more. Though her neighbors tell her she should work harder, smarter and more efficiently than physically possible, she rounds up all her goats and sells them in a nearby town. Capable knows she’s tried her best and her best didn’t work. She decides to take up fishing; something that no one in Frip has done for quite a long time. <br /><br />The gappers are forced to move onto the next family’s goats, those that belong to the Romo’s. Evidently, it had not occurred to the other families that the gappers would be back to taunt their goats once Capable’s goats were gone. With the tables turned, the Romos now look to the Ronsens for help. The Ronsens, clearly not anticipating what is to come, refuse to help. <br /><br />After a series of ridiculous (yet true to life) strategies to rid themselves of the gappers, the Romos and the Ronsens find themselves in dire circumstances. Capable, initially pleased to see the families get their comeuppance, takes pity and invites them for dinner. Finally seeing the wisdom of Capable’s ways, the Romos and the Ronsens decide to sell their goats, and take up fishing. And things in this small seaside town get a little better.<br /><br />But what of the gappers? With the all the goats gone they need to find a new object of devotion to which they can attach themselves and emit their loving shrieks. They soon find something perfectly suitable to their needs, creating the scene of one of my favorite book endings ever.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11938982199324856513">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_635324509">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/635324509" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/20381057-erin" class="left imgcol" title="Erin"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Erin" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/635324509?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 25, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/20381057-erin" class="user" title="Erin">Erin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/28748.The_Very_Persistent_Gappers_of_Frip" title="Hardcover 9781932416374" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/20381057-erin?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer635324509">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11594542622837755530"><em>The Very Persistent Gappers of Frip</em>. It's all in the title, isn't it? This little book is a snappy satire masquerading as a children's book. A cast of twisted characters and strange (or mortified) creatures challenge our heroine Capable, who has to make sense out of a way of life that has gone utterly (udderly?) topsy-turvy. <br /><br />Children will find the text delightfully weird and will enjoy the beautifully colored and textured illustrations. So will adults, for that matter. In fact, adults may be sur</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11594542622837755530"><em>The Very Persistent Gappers of Frip</em>. It's all in the title, isn't it? This little book is a snappy satire masquerading as a children's book. A cast of twisted characters and strange (or mortified) creatures challenge our heroine Capable, who has to make sense out of a way of life that has gone utterly (udderly?) topsy-turvy. <br /><br />Children will find the text delightfully weird and will enjoy the beautifully colored and textured illustrations. So will adults, for that matter. In fact, adults may be surprised by how relevant and thought-provoking the themes of the story are. I was especially struck by Capable's quest to find value in (and harmony with) time, work, and the expenditure of energy. As for the artwork, there are more than a few pages from this book I'd love to have framed on my wall.<br /><br />Honestly, there's nothing about this book I don't like and I happily recommend it to any who ask. ...Sometimes I even recommend it to those who don't.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11594542622837755530">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_38142562">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/38142562" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1133758-adam" class="left imgcol" title="Adam"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1394776951p2/1133758.jpg" alt="Adam" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/38142562?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 19, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1133758-adam" class="user" title="Adam">Adam</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1133758-adam?shelf=evil-kids-books" class="actionLinkLite">evil-kids-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer38142562">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14025830925063186837">This is, in a way, a variation on the classic children’s story <em>The Little Red Hen</em>. Unfortunately, it is also heavy-handed, deeply cynical, and mean-spirited. The author has a very low view of ordinary people and obviously takes great delight in depicting most of the characters here as despicable, narrow-minded fools. Only one small put-upon girl, Capable, and her late, sainted mother are shown to be decent folk--and they come off as near-perfect. So, yes, it’s a story of extremes, and simplistic</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14025830925063186837">This is, in a way, a variation on the classic children’s story <em>The Little Red Hen</em>. Unfortunately, it is also heavy-handed, deeply cynical, and mean-spirited. The author has a very low view of ordinary people and obviously takes great delight in depicting most of the characters here as despicable, narrow-minded fools. Only one small put-upon girl, Capable, and her late, sainted mother are shown to be decent folk--and they come off as near-perfect. So, yes, it’s a story of extremes, and simplistic to boot ("There are good people and bad people..."). And Capable’s jerk neighbors are, of course, very religious folk because--God knows--that’s always a telltale sign of a loser.<br /><br />Sorry, but I wouldn’t feed this book to the dog of my worst enemy… that is, should I ever acquire a worst enemy… and should he or she have a dog.<br /><br />[shelf: <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/list/1133758?shelf=evil-kids-books">evil-kids-books</a>]</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14025830925063186837">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1444203456">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1444203456" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/439937-jim" class="left imgcol" title="Jim"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1328297665p2/439937.jpg" alt="Jim" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1444203456?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 02, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/439937-jim" class="user" title="Jim">Jim</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/28748.The_Very_Persistent_Gappers_of_Frip" title="Hardcover 9781932416374" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/439937-jim?shelf=young-adult-child" class="actionLinkLite">young-adult-child</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/439937-jim?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1444203456">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11578497046829002608">A delightful, if not a slightly creepy, allegory mostly aimed at children, of selfishness, narrow mindedness, and uncooperative behavior being broken down by kindness. Illustrated by Lane Smith with sometimes disturbing graphical images, though well done and occasionally humorous. I'm not sure how children would react to it, but it is a worthwhile tale. Not as funny as some of Saunders' short stories. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14489004482106382760">I thought my George Saunders collection would remain incomplete for this delightful little book is currently POS. Luckily, my recent visit to Powell's-Portland proved fruitful and I walked away with a lovely copy of this illustrated tale. Very much in the vein of a fable or folktalke, Saunders and Smith combine their magics making this one rare potion and a delicious treat. It's odd, yet thoughtful. And wild, yet sane. It's no surprise that I'd love this one. The duo is a talented one and I've y</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14489004482106382760">I thought my George Saunders collection would remain incomplete for this delightful little book is currently POS. Luckily, my recent visit to Powell's-Portland proved fruitful and I walked away with a lovely copy of this illustrated tale. Very much in the vein of a fable or folktalke, Saunders and Smith combine their magics making this one rare potion and a delicious treat. It's odd, yet thoughtful. And wild, yet sane. It's no surprise that I'd love this one. The duo is a talented one and I've yet to be disappointed by either one. Would make a great family read for those with little ones 5 &amp; up.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17797798170149303402">I've always been delighted by the illustrations of <a title="Lane Smith" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/23573.Lane_Smith">Lane Smith</a>, especially his zany work on <a title="The Stinky Cheese Man And Other Fairly Stupid Tales by Jon Scieszka" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/407429.The_Stinky_Cheese_Man_And_Other_Fairly_Stupid_Tales">The Stinky Cheese Man: And Other Fairly Stupid Tales</a> by <a title="Jon Scieszka" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/27318.Jon_Scieszka">Jon Scieszka</a>. The central character is a youn</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17797798170149303402">I've always been delighted by the illustrations of <a title="Lane Smith" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/23573.Lane_Smith">Lane Smith</a>, especially his zany work on <a title="The Stinky Cheese Man And Other Fairly Stupid Tales by Jon Scieszka" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/407429.The_Stinky_Cheese_Man_And_Other_Fairly_Stupid_Tales">The Stinky Cheese Man: And Other Fairly Stupid Tales</a> by <a title="Jon Scieszka" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/27318.Jon_Scieszka">Jon Scieszka</a>. So when my wonderful daughter told me about <a title="The Very Persistent Gappers of Frip by George Saunders" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28748.The_Very_Persistent_Gappers_of_Frip">The Very Persistent Gappers of Frip</a> by <a title="George Saunders" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8885.George_Saunders">George Saunders</a>, which is has 52 illustrations by Smith, I immediately ordered it from the library. <br /><br />It only took me 20 minutes to read the 84 pages--yet "delight" is the word that stays with me for this slim, surreal volume. The central character is a young girl named Capable: <img class="escapedImg" />, who is endearing despite all her travails. I like this blurb from Amazon:<br /><br />"The townspeople of Frip make their living raising goats, but they must fight off a daily invasion of gappers, bright orange, many-eyed creatures that cover goats and stop them from giving milk. When the gappers target Capable's goats, the Romos and the Ronsens turn their backs on the gapper-ridden Capable. What will Capable do about her gapper plague? An imaginative tale by acclaimed author George Saunders accented with haunting illustrations by award-winning illustrator Lane Smith, <em>The Very Persistent Gappers of Frip</em> is an adult story for children, a children's story for adults, an oceanside fable for the landlocked, a fish story for loaves, and a fable about the true meaning of community."<br /><br />I'll just share one more of Smith's enchanting illustrations, this one of the three houses of Capable, the Romos and the Ronsens: <img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1452355426i/17683928.jpg" /><br /><br />I totally agree with this Amazon reviewer: "I stumbled across this book in the store, sat down with it... read the whole thing, laughed... laughed some more... thought of many people I want to give this book to... and ended up purchasing five copies. One will be for my own re-reading." :-)</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1480113480">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1480113480" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/78792-cat" class="left imgcol" title="Cat"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1199044332p2/78792.jpg" alt="Cat" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1480113480?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 26, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/78792-cat" class="user" title="Cat">Cat</a>

          added it


          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/28748.The_Very_Persistent_Gappers_of_Frip" title="Hardcover 9781932416374" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1480113480">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14771208194843888399">I love George Saunders. <a title="Tenth of December by George Saunders" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13641208.Tenth_of_December">Tenth of December</a> blew me away last year, and he came and spoke at my university this past spring. He was as droll and insightful as you would imagine from the dark humor of his prose, but he was also very humble and centered and warm--not at all the apocalyptic sensibility that comes through in his fiction (though that's clearly a dark vein running under the surface). His tips about writing were so practical that they were helpful no matter what your metier, whether fict</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14771208194843888399">I love George Saunders. <a title="Tenth of December by George Saunders" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13641208.Tenth_of_December">Tenth of December</a> blew me away last year, and he came and spoke at my university this past spring. He was as droll and insightful as you would imagine from the dark humor of his prose, but he was also very humble and centered and warm--not at all the apocalyptic sensibility that comes through in his fiction (though that's clearly a dark vein running under the surface). His tips about writing were so practical that they were helpful no matter what your metier, whether fiction (like him) or scholarly writing (like me).<br /><br />Anyway, suffice it to say that I am a huge Saunders fan, and this picture book did not disappoint. The illustrations by Lane Smith are absolutely brilliant. Imagine Marc Chagall doing Quentin Blake cartoons. (They really didn't look at all like Blake, but Smith tackles some of the caricatured, self-absorbed characters in Saunders' fable, and those are very much like the grotesques perfected in the Roald Dahl and Quentin Blake collaboration.) Shocks of color, funny faces, a beautiful, layered sense of texture. Each page I felt like I could fall into, yet the dialogue and the story were carrying me onward.<br /><br />When he came to campus, Saunders said that many of his stories begin with a voice, and you could really feel that in <em>The Very Persistent Gappers of Frip</em>. He absolutely nails that sort of Fox News self-righteous sensibility that assumes that prosperity must be a reward for virtue. A playful and funny tale, but one that also hits home, particularly with American (and perhaps first-world more broadly construed) misconceptions about inequality. I look forward to sharing this with my daughter when she is a little older and has developed her sense of irony more keenly (or at all--three year olds are many things, but ironic is not one of them).</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14771208194843888399">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1754997903466761767">[read the GR description of this book before continuing below]<br /><br />A wonderful parable and biting commentary about the social implications of bad luck and circumstance, and the sanctimonious attitudes of those who are better off, by nothing of their own doing.<br /><br />Favorite quote:<br /><br />Dear Capable, it said, we are in receipt of your letter of the other day, that other day, whenever that day was, when you sent that letter that you sent us. We regret to inform you that, although we are very sympathetic to your si</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1754997903466761767">[read the GR description of this book before continuing below]<br /><br />A wonderful parable and biting commentary about the social implications of bad luck and circumstance, and the sanctimonious attitudes of those who are better off, by nothing of their own doing.<br /><br />Favorite quote:<br /><br />Dear Capable, it said, we are in receipt of your letter of the other day, that other day, whenever that day was, when you sent that letter that you sent us. We regret to inform you that, although we are very sympathetic to your significant hardships, don't you think it would be better if you took responsibility for your own life? We feel strongly that, once you rid your goats of gappers, as we have, you will feel better about yourself, and also, we will feel better about you. Not that we're saying we're better than you, necessarily, it's just that, since gappers are bad, and since you and you alone now have them, it only stands to reason that you are not, perhaps, quite as good as us. Not that we hate you! We sort of even like you. Just please get rid of those gappers! Prove that you can do it, and at that time, and that time only, please come over and won't that be fun, all of us standing around the fire, sharing a laugh about those bad old days when we all had gappers.<br />Love, Your Neighbors.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1063831638">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1063831638" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2622581-yexxo" class="left imgcol" title="yexxo"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1314986822p2/2622581.jpg" alt="yexxo" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1063831638?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 25, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2622581-yexxo" class="user" title="yexxo">yexxo</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1291304.Die_furchtbar_hartn_ckigen_Gapper_von_Frip" title="Hardcover 9783827050335" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2622581-yexxo?shelf=permanent" class="actionLinkLite">permanent</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2622581-yexxo?shelf=kinder" class="actionLinkLite">kinder</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1063831638">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16484302363751809851">Es ist eine völlig skurrile Geschichte mit nicht einmal ganz so unsympathischen 'Unholden', den Gappern. Aus lauter Liebe heften sie sich an Ziegen, die jedoch alles andere als begeistert davon sind und früher oder später aufhören, Milch zu geben und immer dünner werden. Die kleinen Kinder in dem Dörfchen Frip, das aus gerade mal drei Häusern besteht, haben die Aufgabe die Ziegen von den Gappern zu befreien, um sie anschließend ins Meer zu werfen. Doch drei Stunden später haben sie sich mühsam w</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16484302363751809851">Es ist eine völlig skurrile Geschichte mit nicht einmal ganz so unsympathischen 'Unholden', den Gappern. Aus lauter Liebe heften sie sich an Ziegen, die jedoch alles andere als begeistert davon sind und früher oder später aufhören, Milch zu geben und immer dünner werden. Die kleinen Kinder in dem Dörfchen Frip, das aus gerade mal drei Häusern besteht, haben die Aufgabe die Ziegen von den Gappern zu befreien, um sie anschließend ins Meer zu werfen. Doch drei Stunden später haben sie sich mühsam wieder an die Ziegen herangekämpft ('...wenn du so groß bist wie ein Tennisball und keine Beine hast und dich fortbewegst, indem du deinen äußerst empfindlichen Bauch zusammen- und wieder auseinanderkrumpelst...'), um ihnen erneut ihre Liebe zu zeigen und die Kinder beginnen wieder von vorne. Doch als einer der etwas intelligenteren Gapper feststellt, dass es zu den Ziegen von Serena etwas näher ist als zu den anderen, bleibt die ganze Gruppe dort und der Rest wird verschont. Nun muss sie alleine alle Gapper entfernen, denn ihre Nachbarn sind der Meinung, dass es wohl ihre eigene Schuld sein muss und aalen sich in der Überzeugung, selbst etwas Besseres zu sein bzw. etwas Tolles vollbracht zu haben, weshalb sie von der Plage verschont bleiben. Doch Serena akzeptiert ihr Schicksal nicht und überlegt sich einen neuen Weg...<br />Ich finde diese Geschichte ungemein unterhaltsam und lehrreich (ohne erhobenen Zeigefinger), denn sie transportiert derart viele Botschaften, dass dieses Büchlein in jedem Fall mehrmals gelesen werden sollte. Es geht um Selbstbewusstsein, zu lernen den eigenen Weg zu gehen auch gegen den Willen der Mehrheit. Um Liebe, die auch unglücklich machen kann. Um Nächstenliebe, die man nicht nur den Menschen angedeihen lassen soll, die man mag, sondern wirklich allen und die einen auch selbst glücklich macht. Um Egoismus, der aber nur kurzfristig Vorteile bringen mag. Und bestimmt noch um einiges mehr, was mir jetzt aber gerade nicht einfällt :wink: Auch die Bebilderung des Buches ist so ungewöhnlich wie die Geschichte. Es sind Zeichnungen, die sehr plakativ wirken, manche erscheinen wie Collagen, andere wie richtige Gemälde. Die Kinder mit denen ich das Buch gelesen habe, möchten diese schiefen und überspitzten Illustrationen, die die Eigenheiten der Geschichte überdeutlich darstellten.<br />Einziges Manko: Ein größeres Format hätte diesen Bildern sicherlich gut getan. Aber auch so hat dieses Büchlein einen festen Platz in meinem Regal.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16484302363751809851">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1467599894">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1467599894" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/112040-harris" class="left imgcol" title="Harris"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1440690251p2/112040.jpg" alt="Harris" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1467599894?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 18, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/112040-harris" class="user" title="Harris">Harris</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/28748.The_Very_Persistent_Gappers_of_Frip" title="Hardcover 9781932416374" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/112040-harris?shelf=read-aloud" class="actionLinkLite">read-aloud</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/112040-harris?shelf=juvenile-and-young-adult" class="actionLinkLite">juvenile-and-young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1467599894">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16425746934885056419">Last week, I attended the Rain Taxi Review of Book's 20th Anniversary celebration at Macalester College in St. Paul <a rel="nofollow" href="https://mspadventuretime.wordpress.com/2015/12/09/msp-reading-time-rain-taxi-and-george-saunders/">(read more about it on my blog, MSP Adventure Time),</a> and the guest of honor was George Saunders, introduced as "one of the best writers on the planet." I would say that's not hyperbole. In addition to discussing his inspiring philosophies on writing and storytelling, he also read from this book, celebrating the publication of its 15th anniversary reprint. For those in the audience</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16425746934885056419">Last week, I attended the Rain Taxi Review of Book's 20th Anniversary celebration at Macalester College in St. Paul <a rel="nofollow" href="https://mspadventuretime.wordpress.com/2015/12/09/msp-reading-time-rain-taxi-and-george-saunders/">(read more about it on my blog, MSP Adventure Time),</a> and the guest of honor was George Saunders, introduced as "one of the best writers on the planet." I would say that's not hyperbole. In addition to discussing his inspiring philosophies on writing and storytelling, he also read from this book, celebrating the publication of its 15th anniversary reprint. For those in the audience who had not been introduced to the humane, funny, and deceptively deep writing of George Saunders, this picture book for adults and children alike may be the very best place to start.<br /><br />Illustrated by the quirky and idiosyncratic Lane Smith (a great compliment to Saunders' writing), this story follows the seaside goat herding community of Frip and its problems with gappers, an aquatic pest with an all abiding love of goats and an annoying screech of affection. As young Capable, who, like all children of Frip spends much of her time brushing gappers off of the goats and dumping them into the sea after which they crawl slowly back, finds herself the recipient of all the gappers in Frip, her community begins to fracture. This tale of cooperation, ingenuity, and resistance to change has a lot of parallels with much current debate in the world- simple yet full of familiar details relevent to our own lives, and full of great wit. Listening to George Saunders read it to the audience was particularly awesome.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16425746934885056419">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }

    <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/8885.George_Saunders">About George Saunders</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/8885.George_Saunders"><img alt="8885" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1355356844p2/8885.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer12166903686000873985">George Saunders was born December 2, 1958 and raised on the south side of Chicago. In 1981 he received a B.S. in Geophysical Engineering from Colorado School of Mines in Golden, Colorado. He worked at Radian International, an environmental engineering firm in Rochester, NY as a technical writer and geophysical engineer from 1989 to 1996. He has also worked in Sumatra on an oil exploration geophysi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12166903686000873985">George Saunders was born December 2, 1958 and raised on the south side of Chicago. In 1981 he received a B.S. in Geophysical Engineering from Colorado School of Mines in Golden, Colorado. He worked at Radian International, an environmental engineering firm in Rochester, NY as a technical writer and geophysical engineer from 1989 to 1996. He has also worked in Sumatra on an oil exploration geophysics crew, as a doorman in Beverly Hills, a roofer in Chicago, a convenience store clerk, a guitarist in a Texas country-and-western band, and a knuckle-puller in a West Texas slaughterhouse. <br /><br />After reading in <em>People</em> magazine about the Master's program at Syracuse University, he applied. Mr. Saunders received an MA with an emphasis in creative writing in 1988. His thesis advisor was Doug Unger.<br /><br />He has been an Assistant Professor, Syracuse University Creative Writing Program since 1997. He has also been a Visiting Writer at Vermont Studio Center, University of Georgia MayMester Program, University of Denver, University of Texas at Austin, St. Petersburg Literary Seminar (St. Petersburg, Russia, Summer 2000), Brown University, Dickinson College, Hobart &amp; William Smith Colleges. <br /><br />He conducted a Guest Workshop at the Eastman School of Music, Fall 1995, and was an Adjunct Professor at Saint John Fisher College, Rochester, New York, 1990-1995; and Adjunct Professor at Siena College, Loudonville, New York in Fall 1989.<br /><br />He is married and has two children.<br /><br />His favorite charity is a project to educate Tibetan refugee children in Nepal. Information on this can be found at <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.tibetan-buddhist.org/index1.htm" target="_blank">http://www.tibetan-buddhist.org/index...</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12166903686000873985">...more</a>

    </div>
